Ritual variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ racing day ♥ "The concept of entertainments in the ancient and medieval world is problematic. To a modern reader it implies marginal and inessential activities geared to offer pleasure to individuals and groups. In the Graeco-Roman world public performances and spectacles were an essential element in the life of a properly constituted community. In both east and west rich citizens were required to undertake the funding of such events, for the benefit of all their fellow citizens. Moreover, the most important spectacles were provided as part of festivals, held in honour of divinities—including the emperors. In order to ensure that such spectacles were as excellent as possible, they were presented within the framework of a contest. Not only did sportsmen compete in athletic activities, but plays, recitals, and musical entertainments were presented within a competitive structure, which both ensured the production of excellence, and also emphasized the importance of victory: this latter aspect came to have increasing prominence, and to be stressed and appropriated by rulers." [426] "Chariot races were the most important events in the life of the ordinary Byzantine citizen, and race days were occasions for excessive gambling, roistering, eating, and shouting-providing a particular kind of excitement and entertainment not available to him in any other facet of his life. The spectator chose a circus colour, and supported it for the sake of the supporting; 'and he shoved, screamed and lost his temper for the pleasure of it, for the intoxication of taking sides.' As a colour partisan, the Byzantine gambled his self-esteem on the outcome of the race, and this enthusiastic backing of a racing colour gave him an opportunity for contest, rivalry, and risk that was otherwise denied him in an increasingly authoritarian society." [427] "During the mid-day interval of a typical racing programme, spectators remained in their seats and were entertained by a wide variety of performers. A series of individual acts or turns were presented: these included dancers, wild beasts, clowns, and on occasion, some of the spectators themselves. Mimes and singers performed, and acrobats walked a tightrope strung high above the circus floor. It is not certain how these activities evolved over the years, but they were recorded as an important and popular part of the festivities during the tenth century." [428] NOTE: It was difficult to choose between this ritual and the imperial procession (described, for example, by McCormick [429]--but it does seem that chariot races attracted the largest number of people to a single location.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ semi-annually closest magnitude we have to this in the Code Book at the moment is yearly "The fixed chariot races only took place three times a year; but they were also put on to mark special events, such as imperial victories (McCormick 1986)." [430]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ semi-annually closest magnitude we have to this in the Code Book at the moment is yearly "The fixed chariot races only took place three times a year; but they were also put on to mark special events, such as imperial victories (McCormick 1986)." [431]

♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ "Attendance was apparently restricted to men. Cameron noted that the kathisma was surmounted by a room with grilled windows, from behind which the ladies of the court could watch the events below, 'it not being considered proper for ladies to attend the games in the ordinary way.' In a reference to factional rivalry, Procopius commented that women joined factions, although they never even went to the games." [435]

Records refer to fights between these colour partisans that escalated from simple faction rivalry into full-scale rioting. The most serious riot in the hippodrome was the infamous Nika Revolt of 532, which began as a typical faction riot, developed into a major disturbance, and finally ended with the ruthless slaughter of 30,000 people trapped in the hippodrome." [454]

Cohesion
♠ honour code ♣ inferred present ♥ Stronger bond between ruler and ruled. "When a winning charioteer moved forward to receive his prizes from the emperor after the race, the charioteer’s supporting faction would chant to the emperor: 'We ask for equal share of your victory that comes from God, an equal share of your victory, Master, the faith of the kings prevails.' Thus, the charioteer’s victory is seen as but a victory of the emperor. With this hippodrome ritual, the emperor’s image and sovereignty were reinforced, and at the same time, the people were able to acclaim their leader." [465]
♠ oath taking ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this is not mentioned by the sources [466][467].
♠ enemies of any group member are my enemies ♣ present ♥ Winner's victory seen as metaphor for emperor's victory over other polities. "A fundamental feature of the imperial cult was the significance of victory. Victory of any kind, anywhere in the Empire, was associated with the emperor and became his victory; conversely, all victories emanated from the emperor, as the earthly representative of God. By the sixth century, the victory cult of the emperor had become firmly established as part of the ritual of the hippodrome; victories in chariot races were linked with the emperor, and this connection was reflected in the imperial ceremony of the circus." [468]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ everyday church service ♥ "It goes without saying that the Byzantine countryside was Christian in its belief and rituals. Rural paganism was still alive here and there in the sixth century, but it was later eradicated leaving behind merely a few vestigial traces: ancient gods transformed into demons (and sometimes into saints?), festivals, fortunetelling. [...] But these festivals were mere deviations from the established rituals, and their participants, whether peasants or Constantinopolitans, considered themselves orthodox Christians, attended the liturgy, and partook of the Eucharist in their churches." [489] "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day. What, indeed, could be pleasanter than attendance at church? Instead of diabolical songs and dancing whores, it provided the warbling of the Prophets, the chanting of the Seraphim, the words of the Gospel. Christ was there, lying on the altar table, the Holy Spirit was in attendance. The church was like a calm harbour in the midst of turmoil. Yet people had to be cajoled to go there. They found the service boring, and only the reputation of a famous preacher would draw them to church in their hundreds. But after applauding the sermon as in a theatre, they did not even bother to remain for the liturgy of the faithful." [490] In the absence of a detailed description of daily church service, information from the preceding quote may be supplemented by the following information on Byzantine liturgical ceremonies more generally. "Liturgical ceremonies involve the symbolic use of sensible objects such as bread, wine, water, oil, salt, candles, incense, icons, furnishings (altar, baptismal font), vesture (ecclesiastical costume, baptismal robe), edifices (church, baptistery, skeuophylakeion), and ritual gestures, or actions such as anointing, blessing, signing, bathing or washing, imposition of hands, touching, kissing, dressing or stripping, eating, processions, proskynesis, kneeling, and other postures. [...] The liturgy was usually presided over by a minister in priestly orders (bishop or presbyter) and directed by a deacon who regulated the gestures and posture of the congregation via instructions (diakonika) and announced the intentions of their prayer (litany). [...] Liturgical ceremonies contain both fixed and variable elements. The 'ordinary' is the basic skeleton that remains invariable regardless of the day, feast, or season. The texts of the ordinary express a service's changeless purpose; for example, vespers is always evening prayer. The 'proper' comprises those pieces (lections, hymns, psalmody, refrains, etc.) that vary with the day, feast, or season. Christmas Vespers is evening prayer in commemoration of the Nativity. The texts of the proper are contained in a variety of different liturgical books." [491]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day." [492]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day." [493]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day." [496] "Women were excluded from the clergy, except for the order of deaconesses which survived until the twelfth century. Deaconesses originally assisted with the baptism of women at the time when adult baptism by immersion was customary, and then evolved into a group of women who performed charitable services, functioning as social workers and visiting nurses." [497]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ everyday church service ♥ "It goes without saying that the Byzantine countryside was Christian in its belief and rituals. Rural paganism was still alive here and there in the sixth century, but it was later eradicated leaving behind merely a few vestigial traces: ancient gods transformed into demons (and sometimes into saints?), festivals, fortunetelling. [...] But these festivals were mere deviations from the established rituals, and their participants, whether peasants or Constantinopolitans, considered themselves orthodox Christians, attended the liturgy, and partook of the Eucharist in their churches." [530] "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day. What, indeed, could be pleasanter than attendance at church? Instead of diabolical songs and dancing whores, it provided the warbling of the Prophets, the chanting of the Seraphim, the words of the Gospel. Christ was there, lying on the altar table, the Holy Spirit was in attendance. The church was like a calm harbour in the midst of turmoil. Yet people had to be cajoled to go there. They found the service boring, and only the reputation of a famous preacher would draw them to church in their hundreds. But after applauding the sermon as in a theatre, they did not even bother to remain for the liturgy of the faithful." [531] In the absence of a detailed description of daily church service, information from the preceding quote may be supplemented by the following information on Byzantine liturgical ceremonies more generally. "Liturgical ceremonies involve the symbolic use of sensible objects such as bread, wine, water, oil, salt, candles, incense, icons, furnishings (altar, baptismal font), vesture (ecclesiastical costume, baptismal robe), edifices (church, baptistery, skeuophylakeion), and ritual gestures, or actions such as anointing, blessing, signing, bathing or washing, imposition of hands, touching, kissing, dressing or stripping, eating, processions, proskynesis, kneeling, and other postures. [...] The liturgy was usually presided over by a minister in priestly orders (bishop or presbyter) and directed by a deacon who regulated the gestures and posture of the congregation via instructions (diakonika) and announced the intentions of their prayer (litany). [...] Liturgical ceremonies contain both fixed and variable elements. The 'ordinary' is the basic skeleton that remains invariable regardless of the day, feast, or season. The texts of the ordinary express a service's changeless purpose; for example, vespers is always evening prayer. The 'proper' comprises those pieces (lections, hymns, psalmody, refrains, etc.) that vary with the day, feast, or season. Christmas Vespers is evening prayer in commemoration of the Nativity. The texts of the proper are contained in a variety of different liturgical books." [532]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day." [533]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day." [534]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "Above all, a Christian was to go to church--not only on Sundays and other feast days, but as often as possible and at least two hours a day." [535] Thomas Cressy: As all Christians were expected to perform the ritual, it seems there were no passive spectators for this, so I coded this variable as 'not applicable' instead of 'daily'.

"adult males The liturgy was usually presided over by a minister in priestly orders (bishop or presbyter) and directed by a deacon who regulated the gestures and posture of the congregation via instructions (diakonika) and announced the intentions of their prayer (litany)." [537] "Women were excluded from the clergy, except for the order of deaconesses which survived until the twelfth century. Deaconesses originally assisted with the baptism of women at the time when adult baptism by immersion was customary, and then evolved into a group of women who performed charitable services, functioning as social workers and visiting nurses." [538]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ racing day ♥ "The concept of entertainments in the ancient and medieval world is problematic. To a modern reader it implies marginal and inessential activities geared to offer pleasure to individuals and groups. In the Graeco-Roman world public performances and spectacles were an essential element in the life of a properly constituted community. In both east and west rich citizens were required to undertake the funding of such events, for the benefit of all their fellow citizens. Moreover, the most important spectacles were provided as part of festivals, held in honour of divinities—including the emperors. In order to ensure that such spectacles were as excellent as possible, they were presented within the framework of a contest. Not only did sportsmen compete in athletic activities, but plays, recitals, and musical entertainments were presented within a competitive structure, which both ensured the production of excellence, and also emphasized the importance of victory: this latter aspect came to have increasing prominence, and to be stressed and appropriated by rulers." [571] "Chariot races were the most important events in the life of the ordinary Byzantine citizen, and race days were occasions for excessive gambling, roistering, eating, and shouting-providing a particular kind of excitement and entertainment not available to him in any other facet of his life. The spectator chose a circus colour, and supported it for the sake of the supporting; 'and he shoved, screamed and lost his temper for the pleasure of it, for the intoxication of taking sides.' As a colour partisan, the Byzantine gambled his self-esteem on the outcome of the race, and this enthusiastic backing of a racing colour gave him an opportunity for contest, rivalry, and risk that was otherwise denied him in an increasingly authoritarian society." [572] "During the mid-day interval of a typical racing programme, spectators remained in their seats and were entertained by a wide variety of performers. A series of individual acts or turns were presented: these included dancers, wild beasts, clowns, and on occasion, some of the spectators themselves. Mimes and singers performed, and acrobats walked a tightrope strung high above the circus floor. It is not certain how these activities evolved over the years, but they were recorded as an important and popular part of the festivities during the tenth century." [573] NOTE: It was difficult to choose between this ritual and the imperial procession (described, for example, by McCormick [574]--but it does seem that chariot races attracted the largest number of people to a single location.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ semi-annually closest magnitude we have to this in the Code Book at the moment is yearly "The fixed chariot races only took place three times a year; but they were also put on to mark special events, such as imperial victories (McCormick 1986)." [575]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ semi-annually closest magnitude we have to this in the Code Book at the moment is yearly "The fixed chariot races only took place three times a year; but they were also put on to mark special events, such as imperial victories (McCormick 1986)." [576]

♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 60,000 ♥ "Estimates of spectator seating range from 40,000 to 80,000 with 60,000 the most accepted figure. Bury stated that 'it is not unlikely that the area occupied by the seats was larger in the Hippodrome than in the Circus Maximus.'" [579]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ adult males. "Attendance was apparently restricted to men. Cameron noted that the kathisma was surmounted by a room with grilled windows, from behind which the ladies of the court could watch the events below, 'it not being considered proper for ladies to attend the games in the ordinary way.' In a reference to factional rivalry, Procopius commented that women joined factions, although they never even went to the games." [580]

♠ risk of death ♣ present ♥ "Byzantine history is liberally sprinkled with accounts of riots, acts of vandal- ism, and violent demonstrations, many of which occurred in the hippodrome, and often involving one or more of the colour factions. Records refer to fights between these colour partisans that escalated from simple faction rivalry into full-scale rioting. The most serious riot in the hippodrome was the infamous Nika Revolt of 532, which began as a typical faction riot, developed into a major disturbance, and finally ended with the ruthless slaughter of 30,000 people trapped in the hippodrome." [599]

Cohesion
♠ honour code ♣ inferred present ♥ Stronger bond between ruler and ruled. "When a winning charioteer moved forward to receive his prizes from the emperor after the race, the charioteer’s supporting faction would chant to the emperor: 'We ask for equal share of your victory that comes from God, an equal share of your victory, Master, the faith of the kings prevails.' Thus, the charioteer’s victory is seen as but a victory of the emperor. With this hippodrome ritual, the emperor’s image and sovereignty were reinforced, and at the same time, the people were able to acclaim their leader." [610]
♠ oath taking ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this is not mentioned by the sources [611][612].
♠ enemies of any group member are my enemies ♣ present ♥ Winner's victory seen as metaphor for emperor's victory over other polities. "A fundamental feature of the imperial cult was the significance of victory. Victory of any kind, anywhere in the Empire, was associated with the emperor and became his victory; conversely, all victories emanated from the emperor, as the earthly representative of God. By the sixth century, the victory cult of the emperor had become firmly established as part of the ritual of the hippodrome; victories in chariot races were linked with the emperor, and this connection was reflected in the imperial ceremony of the circus." [613]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ funerary wailing ♥ "Just as women were the principal figures at the time of the birth of children, as mothers, midwives, and nurses, so women also assumed a prominent role at the time of death of a family member. First of all, they helped to prepare the corpse for burial, washing the body, sprinkling it with fragrant oils and spices, and clothing it. Then, during the wake, they were the chief mourners, demonstrating their grief by wailing, tearing their hair, lacerating their cheeks with their fingernails, beating their breasts, and ripping apart their garments. Not only female relatives of the deceased, but also hired professional female mourners sang dirges, praising the virtues and lamenting the death of the departed one. Women mourners continued their wailing and lamentations as they accompanied the corpse to the cemetery. This practice elicited criticism by church fathers who complained that the mourning women in their paroxysms of grief resembled Maenads in a Bacchic frenzy and were indulging in shameful behavior by uncovering their heads and tearing their garments so as to reveal portions of their bodies. The church urged that funeral processions be conducted in a solemn, dignified manner and provided trained choirs of men and women to sing psalms and funeral hymns." [634]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ If we consider professional mourners and singers as ritual specialists, it seems likely that they would have presided over funerals less frequently than every day, but more often than monthly. RA's guess.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ At this time the Code Book only has magnitudes of monthly and yearly so coding yearly. semi-annually Difficult to estimate how many deaths a family would suffer--semi-annually (that is, slightly more frequent than yearly) seems like a good guess. RA's guess.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ monthly ♥ If we consider "the people of Byzantium" as this ritual's audience (at least part of the proceedings were public), as well as less-emotionally-involved relatives and friends, it seems likely that they would have seen funerals less frequently than every day, but more often than monthly. RA's guess. changing to monthly - frequency would depend on what part of the polity you lived in rural/urban. Also, if you lived in city, how noisy/packed with people it was the time procession passed might affect whether there was truly an "audience."
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Source does not provide absolute numbers.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ adult women; whole polity. Not only female relatives of the deceased, but also hired professional female mourners sang dirges, praising the virtues and lamenting the death of the departed one. [...] The church urged that funeral processions be conducted in a solemn, dignified manner and provided trained choirs of men and women to sing psalms and funeral hymns." [635]. Thomas Cressy: Rather than coding, {adult women; whole polity}, he seemed better to code as substantial minority to encompass female relatives and the professional singers as there is no academic debate cited. It seems more to be a dilemma of coding than a conflict in expert opinions, so I feel this is the best solution.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ This appears to be the common funerary rite. Thomas Cressy, based on the quote: Not only female relatives of the deceased, but also hired professional female mourners sang dirges, praising the virtues and lamenting the death of the departed one. [...] The church urged that funeral processions be conducted in a solemn, dignified manner and provided trained choirs of men and women to sing psalms and funeral hymns." [636]. it seems that the actual people participating were not the whole polity but a substantial minority and have coded as such. The whole polity seem to be able to spectate, but the participants were professional singers or relatives only.

The church urged that funeral processions be conducted in a solemn, dignified manner and provided trained choirs of men and women to sing psalms and funeral hymns." ♠ Impeachment ♣ absent ♥ Once formally crowned Emperor, the ruler was changed either through death - natural or violent - or abdication. No mention of a legal mechanism. "Of the 109 sovereigns twenty-three ended by being assassinated. Twelve died in a convent or prison. Three died of starvation. Eighteen were mutilated by castration, or had their eyes gouged, or nose or hands cut off; and, apart the thirty-four who died in their beds, and eight that were killed in a war or accident, the others were poisoned, suffocated, strangled, stabbed, hurled from the top of a pillar, or driven away ignominiously. Altogether, within 1058 years, there were sixty-five palace-, street-, or barracks revolutions, and sixty-five dethronements."[695]

♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 7 ♥ *641* "Herakleios set forth a testament whereby his eldest son Constantine and the minor son of his wife Heraklonas were to be emperors of equal rank. Furthermore he decreed that both emperors had to honor his wife Martina as mother and empress. Since parts of the Byzantine society did not accept this powerful position of Martina there emerged two conflicting parties: one supporting Constantine's claim to the throne by reason of his seniority and another one giving preference to Martina and her son Heraklonas. A few months later Constantine III died and Heraklonas became the sole emperor. Unlike Constantine III who had supported Dyotheletism, Heraklonas and Martina sympathizised with Monotheletism and appointed Monotheletic bishops. However, Heraklonas and his mother were dismissed already in the same year (641). The senate, which after a long period of inactivity had regained power and which was inspired by the uprisings of the Blues, banished them appointed Constance II, the son of Constantine III, the new emperor of Byzantium. This event is of particular importance because it is the first time in Byzantine history that the senate dismissed an emperor. After the dismissal of Heraklonas and the appointment of Constance II the senate continued to play a significant political role in Byzantium."[722] *741* Constantine V had to defeat Artavasdus, commander of the Opsician Theme, which he eventually did with the support of the Anatolic and Thracesian themes after being usurped in Constantinople. The civil war lasted two and a half years.[723] *797* "Leo IV married a Greek girl from Athens, another Irene, who at his death became ruler of the Empire because her son, Constantine VI, proclaimed Emperor from 780 to 797, was a minor. Irene, a woman of great force and ambition, entered into a struggle for power with her son when he attained his majority, and was victorious; she dethroned and blinded her son, and became sole ruler of the Empire (797-802 CE).[724] *811* "The army's surviving officers proclaimed him emperor in the hope that he would recover, but even before he arrived in the capital his legs became paralyzed, and his recovery appeared unlikely. Though Stauracius wanted to be succeeded by his wife, his chief officials preferred his sister's husband, the curoplates Michael Rhangabe. Stauracius's attempt to have Michael blinded only hastened his brother-in-law's proclamation. Stauracius was carried off to a monastery after a reign of just over a month.[725] *812* Challenge to Michael I (the Michael Rhangabe above) who reigned from 811 CE in 812 CE. Soldiers mutinied. "Some tagmatic soldiers even tried to proclaim the blinded sons of Constantine V, but Michael's government cashiered them."[726] *821* "But Leo's victories had made him popular among the army in Asia Minor, and at the news of his murder the Anatolic Theme proclaimed Thomas the Slav, who had once served with both Leo V and Michael II under Bardanes Turcus. With as good a claim to the throne as Michael, Thomas was not really a rebel. The Bucellarian, Paphlagonian, and Cibyrrhaeot themes accepted him at once as Leo's successor and avenger, and at the beginning of 821 a civil war broke out."[727] *855* "Theodora's regency seemed to be a successful one, but it was soon to expire, since Michael III had turned fifteen. To his mother and Theoctistus's despair, the adolescent emperor's main interests were drinking, chariot racing, and his mistress, Eudocia Ingerina. ... instead they married Michael to the blameless but colorless Eudocia Decapolitissa. Annoyed by this forced marriage, Michael allied himself with Theodora's brother Bardas, whom he recalled to the capital after years of disgrace. With Michael's agreement, Bardas had his enemy Theoctistus assassinated. The distraught Theodora raged at her son, who for several months tried vainly to placate her. In early 856 Michael had himself proclaimed sole emperor, officially ending her regency. ... Michael expelled his mother from the palace and confined her to a convent in 857"[728]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ ♥

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ ♥

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ present ♥ The Emperor made high-level appointments.
♠ Indirect ♣ present ♥ Bureaucrats could make lower-level appointments: "It often happened that certain particularly energetic civil servants through their unusual activity in the central departments gave their office far greater importance than really belonged to it. They took great care to ensure that the importance gained by this usurpation of the responsibilities of other departments was retained, and with this in mind they appointed suitable successors, colleagues or men drawn from their own circle of relatives."[736]
♠ Diffused ♣ present ♥ Bureaucrats could make lower-level appointments: "It often happened that certain particularly energetic civil servants through their unusual activity in the central departments gave their office far greater importance than really belonged to it. They took great care to ensure that the importance gained by this usurpation of the responsibilities of other departments was retained, and with this in mind they appointed suitable successors, colleagues or men drawn from their own circle of relatives."[737]
♠ Mixed ♣ present ♥ Bureaucrats could make lower-level appointments: "It often happened that certain particularly energetic civil servants through their unusual activity in the central departments gave their office far greater importance than really belonged to it. They took great care to ensure that the importance gained by this usurpation of the responsibilities of other departments was retained, and with this in mind they appointed suitable successors, colleagues or men drawn from their own circle of relatives."[738]
♠ Governmental control ♣ inferred present ♥ The special department/office of the Logothete of the Dromos may have had powers to remove officials from other government departments, although its powers were mainly directed at the provincial/thematic level. "In order to exercise some effective control over the themes, and particularly to safeguard the general interests of the state against the particularism of individual provinces, the office of logothete tou dromou was created. This high imperial official had the position of a commissioner with extraordinary powers. One of his most important duties was to provide for an army on the march. ... His competence included the supervision of imperial roads and post, and he also had the right to impose on any theme economic measures considered necessary to secure provisioning, maintenance and movement of troops on all routes within the Empire. With such authority this office attained so great an importance that the logothete tou dromou soon became the first minister of the Empire."[739]
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Byzantine Patriarch probably did not have any official powers to remove government officials but certainly exercised a lot of informal influence and at times may have had some de facto control.

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ ♥

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ ♥

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ present ♥ Justinian Code may have defined rights for defendants? "The new legal system, called the Justinian Code, also introduced progressive new legislation. In accordance with Christian ideals of aiding the poor and powerless, Justinian established laws to help slaves and people suffering from debt."[748] This is a right: "Under Byzantine Emperor Justinian's Code of A.D. 533, a "freeman could not be summoned from his house" because it is "everyone's safest place, his refuge, and his shelter."[749] "The court was required to issue its judgment within two years of the trial. Defendants in capital cases might be imprisoned throughout the process. Frivolous lawsuits were discouraged by subjecting the losing plaintiff to the same penalty as the accused. Revenge killings were relatively uncommon. As in many societies, criminal penalties varied with the status of plaintiff and defendant."[750]

Accessibility of the Legal System

♠ ruling elite ♣ present ♥ "As in many societies, criminal penalties varied with the status of plaintiff and defendant. Ranking officials were in the best position to defend themselves or settle out of court."[751] "Elites convicted in capital cases other than treason faced losing their titles, personal property, and being banished to a monastery or remote province. Confiscated assets typically were divided into thirds among the family of the victim, the condemned man's own children, and the monastery to which he was to be confined. Public flogging, mutilation, and execution were remedies applied mainly to members of the lower classes."[752]
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ "Frivolous lawsuits were discouraged by subjecting the losing plaintiff to the same penalty as the accused. Revenge killings were relatively uncommon. As in many societies, criminal penalties varied with the status of plaintiff and defendant."[753]
♠ general population ♣ inferred present ♥ "Frivolous lawsuits were discouraged by subjecting the losing plaintiff to the same penalty as the accused. Revenge killings were relatively uncommon. As in many societies, criminal penalties varied with the status of plaintiff and defendant."[754]
♠ foreign groups ♣ inferred present ♥ "Frivolous lawsuits were discouraged by subjecting the losing plaintiff to the same penalty as the accused. Revenge killings were relatively uncommon. As in many societies, criminal penalties varied with the status of plaintiff and defendant."[755]
♠ ethnic groups ♣ inferred present ♥ "Frivolous lawsuits were discouraged by subjecting the losing plaintiff to the same penalty as the accused. Revenge killings were relatively uncommon. As in many societies, criminal penalties varied with the status of plaintiff and defendant."[756]

Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ inferred absent ♥ "... women of all classes and backgrounds did possess important personal, economic, and property rights, guaranteed by law. A woman retained possession of her dowry, though her husband administered it, and could alienate inherited property; widows retained the right of ownership and administration of family property, including dowry goods. They also had authority over their sons. Judicial acts reveal women appearing in courts to testify and plead successfully for divorce, for the resolution of property disputes, and for control over property. Daughters as well as sons had the right to share the inheritance of their parents and property was transferred along female lines."[758]
♠ Social class ♣ inferred present ♥ "As in many societies, criminal penalties varied with the status of plaintiff and defendant."[759]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Types

♠ Chattel slavery ♣ present ♥ "Slaves were considered private property and could lawfully be disciplined with corporal or even lethal force."[766]
♠ Elite slavery ♣ present ♥ "The imperial eunuchs, the cubicularii (koubikoularioi), also paid for their office. They formed the emperor's escort and were a very influential body in the Palace."[767]
♠ Bonded serfdom ♣ inferred absent ♥ Presumably not very common at this time, if there was any at all. Especially from 10th century when there were large landholders: "Paroikoi are sometimes called “Byzantine serfs,” and the parallel is not entirely false since the paroikos was tied to the land which did not belong to him. The paroikoi are to be distinguished from the free peasants who held land in their own name; from the thirteenth century onward virtually all peasants seem to have been paroikoi."[768]

♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ present ♥ "Application of the death penalty waned over time as successive legal revisions restricted its use. John II Komnenos was widely praised for having permitted no executions during his long reign (1118-43)."[771] Physical mutilation (social outcasting) began to replace death penalty from the 8th century CE.[772]

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Description of the Normative Ideology ♣ ♥

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ present ♥ "The emperor was held to be God's earthly representative and his laws essential for maintaining stability."[773] “This providential man was chosen by God: his coins proclaimed the fact that he was ‘from God’ (‘’ek theou’’). [...] The ‘’basileus’’ was God’s own representative on earth who had inherited the trappings of the cult of his pagan Roman predecessors’ divinity.” [774] Byzantine Emperors were revered in many ways, echoing some of the treatment of eastern Mediterrannean rulers in previous eras as divine or semi-divine figures, showing the close connection between political and religious authority. "Proskynesis of the emperor and his haloed image, the image’s privilege of asylum and placement on church altars, the custom of receiving objects from the emperor with covered hands, silence, incense, and lighted candles in his presence stemmed ultimately from the imperial cult and characterized Byz. rulership.” [775]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though emperors were legitimated by God and were themselves worshipped as sacred beings, the relevant entries of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ [776] [777] do not indicate that they were believed to control supernatural energy.

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though emperors were legitimated by God and were themselves worshipped as sacred beings, the relevant entries of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ [778] [779] do not indicate that they were believed to descend from God.

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though emperors were legitimated by God and were themselves worshipped as sacred beings, the relevant entries of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ [780] [781] do not indicate that they were deified after death.

♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though emperors were legitimated by God and were themselves worshipped as sacred beings, the relevant entries of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ [782] [783] do not indicate that they were deified after death.

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ absent ♥ As Christian rulers, Byzantine emperors avoided explicit worship of themselves or descendents as gods, though they were revered in many ways, echoing some of the treatment of eastern Mediterranean rulers in previous eras as divine or semi-divine figures, showing the close connection between political and religious authority. “In the capital, the emperor’s status as God’s representative on earth maintained and even expanded aspects of the imperial cult, esp. the sacredness of imperial persons and institutions concretized by ceremonies and divinizing epithets. Although Constantine avoided ‘’divus’’ for his person, his successors revived the custom, whence arose the Byz. usage of ‘’theios’’ for the imperial person and institutions and ‘’sakra’’ for documents."[784] Emperors were also frequently depicted as gods in works of art, in frescoes and mosaics, though this appears to be a stylistic representation of reverence for the rulers rather than a declaration of divinity.[785]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ absent ♥ Distinctions between emperor and subjects [786] and between men and women [787].

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ “This providential man was chosen by God: his coins proclaimed the fact that he was ‘from God’ (‘’ek theou’’). His subjects called themselves ‘’douloi’’, which in the time of Thucydides had meant ‘slaves’ but now perhaps connoted something closer to ‘servants’. The ‘’basileus’’ was God’s own representative on earth who had inherited the trappings of the cult of his pagan Roman predecessors’ divinity.” [788]
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ present ♥ “The idea of a Christian oikoumene, a community built on creed and culture, organized as a state, prevented the rise of nationalism, which has been one of the strongest forces in international politics since the nineteenth century. Nationalism and racism were both unknown in Byzantium. In one of the sections of the Byzantine so-called ' national ' epic Digenes Akritas called ' The Song of the Emir ', the Arab enemy of the Byzantines finally marries the daughter of a Byzantine general, admittedly not without having converted to Christianity. The hero of the epic is born from this mixed marriage; no one in Byzantium took offence at that.15 As late as the fourteenth century the important author and politician Theodoros Metochites dreamed of a cosmopolitan organization of the whole world, a kind of precursor of the League of Nations or the United Nations; he called it komopoliteia. Of course Constantinople was to be the centre of this state.” [789]
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ suspected unknown ♥ “It is fair to say that Byzantine society was mysoginist and patriarchal, in our terms, for the prevailing ideology towards women regarded them as inferior beings to men, weak, untrustworthy, and ranked with children, the mentally deranged, and slaves as unfit to give public testimony. They were licentious temptresses, possessing an uncontrolled and uncontrollable sexuality, and their proper place was in the home, away from any form of public life (Brown 1988). Men and male behaviour was the norm; women’s roles were conditioned by this.//This was an ideology based on the teachings of the Church. The Fathers described women as inferior and weak in comparison to men, for they were responsible, via Eve, for the Fall of Man. Women were also liable to either indulge in witchcraft and sorcery or to be possessed by demons. They were credulous to a fault. They also were ever-present sources of temptation, and instruments of the devil. However, thanks to being created in God’s image and thanks to the redeeming actions of the Virgin Mary, women were also perceived by the Church as spiritually equal to men. These two mutually contradictory roles underpin their roles in Byzantine society (Galatariotou 1984-5).” [790] Change to present. James clearly says here that 'women were also perceived by the Church as spiritually equal to men'. This is the ideology. The fact that the practice did not conform to the ideology does not change what the ideology was. Changed to suspected unknown prior to confirmation this should be re-coded present. Note that the Islamic Umayyad and Abbasid Caliphates are currently coded suspected unknown for this variable. If there is uncertainty over what the Qur'an says about women I don't know how there is so much certainly over the position of women in the Christian New Testament. Ed.

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present ♥ Distinctions between emperor and subjects [794] and between men and women [795].

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ present ♥ “As mentioned earlier, the central, basic norm of the Church's attitude toward marriage during the first millennium of Christianity is the idea that the mystical union of God and Israel, of Christ and the Church, is reflected only in a marriage that is perfectly monogamous. The idea is already explicit in St. Paul: widowers are allowed to remarry but are not encouraged to do so (1 Cor. 7:39-40). Early Christian writers show a remarkable consensus in supporting that view: the consensus includes Ter- tullian, Athenagoras, the Shepherd of Hermas, Clement, and Origen. In the fourth century St. John Chrysostom composed a treatise addressed ‘’To a Young Widow’’, encouraging her to remain in her widowhood rather than succumb to human weakness and remarry. // Chrysostom's early contemporary, St. Basil of Caesarea, is the author of numerous letters, a number of which were accepted in the Byzantine Church as normative canonical texts and sanc- tioned as such by the Council in Trullo (692). He writes: "The rule establishes one year of excom- munication for those who marry a second time. Other authorities even require two years. Those who marry a third time are often excommunicated for three or four years. And such a union is not called marriage, but polygamy, or rather punishable fornication ... (noevd(av xExokaoptvriv)." This negative attitude toward second or third marriages is maintained in the ninth century by St. Theodore of Stoudios; recognizing that civil law authorized such unions, he forbids their blessing in church.'5 The canons attributed to Patriarch Nicephorus (806-815)16 also forbid the "crowning" of any marriage except the first.17 This principle, which forbade ecclesiastical sanction in cases of consecutive bigamy and trigamy, must have been challenged first in the case of emperors. In the ninth century Theodore of Stoudios deplores the evil example of Emperor Constantine V Copronymos, who was married three times.” [796]

♠ Ideological thought reinforces prosociality ♣ present ♥ “The Byzantine Empire maintained a network of philanthropic institutions designed to shelter travellers and homeless migrants (‘’xenoi’’), provide free medical care for the ill, nurture orphans, and organize food allotments during famines (Constantelos 1991: 113). [...] Modern scholars agree that Christ’s command to assist the suffering underlay the development of specialized welfare services in Byzantium. According to Matthew’s gospel, Jesus required that Christians feed the hungry, clothe the naked, welcome the stranger, and care for the sick (Matt. 25: 34-5).” [797]

♠ charity ♣ present ♥ “The Byzantine Empire maintained a network of philanthropic institutions designed to shelter travellers and homeless migrants (‘’xenoi’’), provide free medical care for the ill, nurture orphans, and organize food allotments during famines (Constantelos 1991: 113). Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that slaves were widely employed throughout the Byzantine empire, including by the Church, and that there is little evidence for opposition to the practice. “Byzantine society inherited from the Romans the ancient distinction between free human beings and slaves, which basically continued to exist throughout the whole Byzantine millennium. [...] Fundamental criticism of slavery was expressed very rarely and only to a certain degree (the exceptions are Gregory of Nyssa [d. 395, cf. his ‘’Opera’’ V, ‘’In ecclesiasten, or.’’ IV] and later Archbishop Eustathios of Thessalonika [d. c. 1195]), but it remained without any general consequences in practice. [...] Slaves, often including eunuchs, were employed mainly in the imperial palaces and workshops, but also in ecclesiastical properties (monasteries) and private households of more or less wealthy people.” [800] However, it is worth noting that comparatively little is known about the institution of slavery in Byzantium, and many slaves were either prisoners of war or descendants of prisoners of war [801].
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that slaves were widely employed throughout the Byzantine empire, including by the Church, and that there is little evidence for opposition to the practice. “Byzantine society inherited from the Romans the ancient distinction between free human beings and slaves, which basically continued to exist throughout the whole Byzantine millennium. [...] Fundamental criticism of slavery was expressed very rarely and only to a certain degree (the exceptions are Gregory of Nyssa [d. 395, cf. his ‘’Opera’’ V, ‘’In ecclesiasten, or.’’ IV] and later Archbishop Eustathios of Thessalonika [d. c. 1195]), but it remained without any general consequences in practice. [...] Slaves, often including eunuchs, were employed mainly in the imperial palaces and workshops, but also in ecclesiastical properties (monasteries) and private households of more or less wealthy people.” [802] However, it is worth noting that comparatively little is known about the institution of slavery in Byzantium, and many slaves were either prisoners of war or descendants of prisoners of war [803].
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery universally ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that slaves were widely employed throughout the Byzantine empire, including by the Church, and that there is little evidence for opposition to the practice. “Byzantine society inherited from the Romans the ancient distinction between free human beings and slaves, which basically continued to exist throughout the whole Byzantine millennium. [...] Fundamental criticism of slavery was expressed very rarely and only to a certain degree (the exceptions are Gregory of Nyssa [d. 395, cf. his ‘’Opera’’ V, ‘’In ecclesiasten, or.’’ IV] and later Archbishop Eustathios of Thessalonika [d. c. 1195]), but it remained without any general consequences in practice. Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ monotheistic ♥
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ present ♥
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ “God exists without beginning or end, as the creator of the universe, omniscient and omnipotent.” [818]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ “God exists without beginning or end, as the creator of the universe, omniscient and omnipotent.” [819]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ moralizing ♥ The Christian god is "the creator of the universe, omniscient and omnipotent"[820] and he punishes moral transgression: "[A]fter the Resurrection, when God and Jesus appear to pass judgement, one must 'render an account' (Matt. 12:36). Death ends the day during which good works are to be done, and then, 'the night cometh, when no man can work' (John 9:4). Everyone's work will be tried in fire and revealed (1 Cor, 3:12-13). The just, whom God will gather to His right, receive 'life everlasting', the merciless, however, who stand to His left, 'shall go into everlasting punishment' (Matt. 25:46). Everything is decided with death and that gives both life and death the weight of finality."[821]

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code “compound” moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ present ♥ In the Old Testament, "Some sins punishable by death were: murder (Ex 21.12-14), striking or cursing parents (21.15, 17), kidnapping (Ex 21.16), adultery, incest, homosexuality, and bestiality (Lv 20.10-16). Other sins, serious though punishable by death, were: stealing (Ex 21.37-22.3), slandering one's wife (Dt 22.13-19), seducing a virgin (Ex 22.15-16), etc. [...] Effects of Sin. Guilt. As a result of revolt against God, a sense of guilt arose."[822] "Guilt is the state or condition of being at fault (reatus culpae) and so deprived of supernatural life, the absence of the splendor of which is a stain (macula peccati) on the soul, and also the state or condition of being liable to the penalty due in punishment for the fault."[823]
♠ supernatural enforcement of fairness ♣ present ♥ "For example, the Gospels attach the promise of a reward to love of enemies (Mt 5.46), almsgiving (Mt 6.1), forgiveness (Mt 6.15), fasting (Mt 6.18), faith (Lk 12.8), perseverance in the face of persecution (Mt 13.18), and following Christ (Mt 11.29)."[824]
♠ supernatural enforcement of human reciprocity ♣ present ♥ "Like all major religions, Christianity made ethical demands. Above all others stood the commandment to charity, in other words to love God and one's neighbour with one's whole soul and mind (see Mark 12: 30-3). Moreover, after the Resurrection, when God and Jesus appear to pass judgement, one must 'render an account' (Matt. 12:36). Death ends the day during which good works are to be done, and then, 'the night cometh, when no man can work' (John 9:4). Everyone's work will be tried in fire and revealed (1 Cor, 3:12-13). The just, whom God will gather to His right, receive 'life everlasting', the merciless, however, who stand to His left, 'shall go into everlasting punishment' (Matt. 25:46). Everything is decided with death and that gives both life and death the weight of finality."[825]

♠ supernatural enforcement of bravery♣ inferred absent ♥ Based on the following quote, it appears that, though bravery in defence of Christianity was seen as praiseworthy, good Christians were not supposed to engage in physical conflict, so that even soldiers, however brave, were sometimes condemned for their actions on the battlefield. “To the Byz., peace and violence were ideals rooted in the teachings of the New Testament and church fathers (esp. St. Basil), but in reality they rarely knew prolonged periods of peace. The Byz. considered war evil, but their attitude was tempered by the recognition of its necessity in defending their Christian empire and brethren; thus courage, prowess in arms, and good generalship were praiseworthy attributes in historical figures such as Herakleios and Basil II, or in such legendary figures as Digenes Akritas. The Byz. also bestowed praise, however, on emperors such as Alexios Komnenos, who avoided unnecessary bloodshed by sparing conquered enemies and using diplomacy to resolve conflicts. Although divine favor in war was sought through military religious service, the cults of warrior saints (see military saints), and prayers for the success of imperial expeditions (Darrouzes, ‘’Epistoliers’’ 146, 149), Byz. churchmen deplored war, esp. between Christians, and refused to sanction killing; Patriarch Polyeuktos countered the petition of Nikephoros II Phokas to have his slain soldiers declared martyrs with St. Basil’s ruling that soldiers who had killed in battle could not receive communion for three years.” [833]
♠ supernatural enforcement of purity♣ present ♥ Sexual purity. In the Old Testament, "Some sins punishable by death were: murder (Ex 21.12-14), striking or cursing parents (21.15, 17), kidnapping (Ex 21.16), adultery, incest, homosexuality, and bestiality (Lv 20.10-16). Other sins, serious though punishable by death, were: stealing (Ex 21.37-22.3), slandering one's wife (Dt 22.13-19), seducing a virgin (Ex 22.15-16), etc. [...] Effects of Sin. Guilt. As a result of revolt against God, a sense of guilt arose."[834] "Guilt is the state or condition of being at fault (reatus culpae) and so deprived of supernatural life, the absence of the splendor of which is a stain (macula peccati) on the soul, and also the state or condition of being liable to the penalty due in punishment for the fault."[835]

♠ other supernatural enforcement of piety♣ present ♥ In the Old Testament, "Some of the more serious sins were: idolatry (Ex 22.19), magic (Ex 22.17), divination (Lv 19.26; etc.), and blasphemy (Lv 24.11-16). [...] Effects of Sin. Guilt. As a result of revolt against God, a sense of guilt arose."[837] "Guilt is the state or condition of being at fault (reatus culpae) and so deprived of supernatural life, the absence of the splendor of which is a stain (macula peccati) on the soul, and also the state or condition of being liable to the penalty due in punishment for the fault."[838] "For example, the Gospels attach the promise of a reward to love of enemies (Mt 5.46), almsgiving (Mt 6.1), forgiveness (Mt 6.15), fasting (Mt 6.18), faith (Lk 12.8), perseverance in the face of persecution (Mt 13.18), and following Christ (Mt 11.29)."[839]
♠ other supernatural enforcement not related to piety ♣ present ♥ Perseverance in the face of persecution. "For example, the Gospels attach the promise of a reward to love of enemies (Mt 5.46), almsgiving (Mt 6.1), forgiveness (Mt 6.15), fasting (Mt 6.18), faith (Lk 12.8), perseverance in the face of persecution (Mt 13.18), and following Christ (Mt 11.29)."[840]

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ present ♥ “The Byzantine Empire maintained a network of philanthropic institutions designed to shelter travellers and homeless migrants (‘’xenoi’’), provide free medical care for the ill, nurture orphans, and organize food allotments during famines (Constantelos 1991: 113). [...] Modern scholars agree that Christ’s command to assist the suffering underlay the development of specialized welfare services in Byzantium. According to Matthew’s gospel, Jesus required that Christians feed the hungry, clothe the naked, welcome the stranger, and care for the sick (Matt. 25: 34-5).” [841]
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ present ♥ “One of Jesus' parables pictured an ‘unjust’ judge who initially was too lazy to hear the case of a widow whom an anonymous person has treated badly over some issue. She is not getting her rights and lacks the support of a husband and family, but her persistent appeals eventually win the day. Her case is heard and she is granted ‘justice’ or what is due to her. The Christian tradition came to call justice, along with prudence, temperance, and fortitude, one of the four cardinal (‘hinge’) virtues, because right human conduct pivots upon them.” [842]
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ present ♥ "The most obvious practice that would have distinguished Christians from pagans was church attendance. Just showing up was a sign of affiliation, however incomplete in the eyes of the Church Fathers. Preachers complained about the decline in attendance on certain days or the laziness of the absent portion of the congregation and praised those who were present for the sermons. In his sermon “Against the Pagans,” Augustine at one point addresses his listeners as the “real Christians,” in contrast to the so-called Christians who had opted for the chariot races. Later, he further acknowledges their presence in church: “Thank heaven that you have come here together at all.”" [843]
♠ respect for authority ♣ present ♥ The emperor’s authority was valued above all. “This providential man was chosen by God: his coins proclaimed the fact that he was ‘from God’ (‘’ek theou’’). His subjects called themselves ‘’douloi’’, which in the time of Thucydides had meant ‘slaves’ but now perhaps connoted something closer to ‘servants’. The ‘’basileus’’ was God’s own representative on earth who had inherited the trappings of the cult of his pagan Roman predecessors’ divinity.” [844]
♠ purity ♣ present ♥ “Chastity is a condition of spiritual and bodily purity in which the Christian person retains control over his or her impulses and desires, presenting a life to God that reflects and realizes the condition of humanity’s first innocent formation. It refers, most often, to the restraining of the specifically sexual appetites of the body, reflecting the word’s Latin root, ‘’castus’’ (‘pure’) which also has from ancient times retained a meaning of abstinence from sexual and other sensual activities. [...] With respect to sexual purity, chastity may refer in practical terms either to a complete abstention from all sexual activity (as in the case of monastics, or as the Orthodox Church expects of all persons prior to marriage), or to the right ordering and sacred engagement of the sexual appetites within the sacramental bond of married life. But chastity as a notion is often employed to the whole spiritual and bodily demeanor of purity before God, and is not simply referring to the sexual appetites. The curbing of any and all passionate impulses may be enjoined under the call to chastity (which is why, for example, it is included as the first positive petition in the famous prayer attributed to St. Ephrem and used throughout the Great Fast); thus chastity may relate to a right practice of patience, godly conversation, the fostering of right social attitudes and activities, the refraining from gossip and slander, etc. [...] Chastity may therefore be seen as the overarching virtue of rightly ordering one’s life and activities, bodily as well as spiritually, so that the other virtues might be realized and God’s temple, the human person, made a fitting home for its King.” [845]
♠ kinship ♣ inferred present ♥ “The warmth of relations between parents and children is often stressed in Byz. literature—in evident disregard for the demans of some rigorists (e.g. the author of the vita of St. Alexio homo Dei) who praised the dissolution of family ties.” [846] At the influential Synod of Gangra (ca. 340-342), "The council’s anathemas were promulgated against the following beliefs: [...](14) that women should abandon their husbands in contempt of marriage; (15) and (16) that parents should abandon children and children abandon their parents for asceticism; [...]"[847]
♠ bravery ♣ inferred absent ♥ Based on the following quote, it appears that, though bravery in defence of Christianity was seen as praiseworthy, good Christians were not supposed to engage in physical conflict, so that even soldiers, however brave, were sometimes condemned for their actions on the battlefield. “To the Byz., peace and violence were ideals rooted in the teachings of the New Testament and church fathers (esp. St. Basil), but in reality they rarely knew prolonged periods of peace. The Byz. considered war evil, but their attitude was tempered by the recognition of its necessity in defending their Christian empire and brethren; thus courage, prowess in arms, and good generalship were praiseworthy attributes in historical figures such as Herakleios and Basil II, or in such legendary figures as Digenes Akritas. The Byz. also bestowed praise, however, on emperors such as Alexios Komnenos, who avoided unnecessary bloodshed by sparing conquered enemies and using diplomacy to resolve conflicts. Although divine favor in war was sought through military religious service, the cults of warrior saints (see military saints), and prayers for the success of imperial expeditions (Darrouzes, ‘’Epistoliers’’ 146, 149), Byz. churchmen deplored war, esp. between Christians, and refused to sanction killing; Patriarch Polyeuktos countered the petition of Nikephoros II Phokas to have his slain soldiers declared martyrs with St. Basil’s ruling that soldiers who had killed in battle could not receive communion for three years.” [848]
♠ respect for property ♣ inferred present ♥ “The church, at least from the 10th century, imposed on thieves severe fasts, compulsory almsgiving, and exclusion from communion for one or two years.” [849]

♠ Moralistic punishment by human agents ♣ present ♥ There were two types of punishment that religious agents or institutions could impose on the faithful: epitimia and excommunication. The epitimion was “a penalty imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession. The term was already in use by the 4th C. (Basil the Great, PG 32:721A). As a rule these penitential exercises, mentioned in Byz. canonical and ascetical literature, presupposed repentance and consisted of prayer, fasting, Scripture reading, prostrations, almsgiving, and, on occasion, temporary exclusion from the Eucharist. They were distinguished by their largely positive character and relative mildness from such formal punishments (‘’timoriai’’) as excommunication, suspension, or deposition, which were inflicted by the church for more serious transgressions such as heresy or apostasy. Since sin was understood as a disease rather than a legally punishable crime, ‘’epitimia’’ in Byz. penitential practice and theology were viewed as corrective remedies, that is, as a form of spiritual healing. At any rate, they were never reduced to a payment or a fine due to God.” [850] As for excommunication, it “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist. Offending members included emperors, for example, Leo VI and Michael VIII. The separation from the church’s sacramental life was either absolute or partial, that is, it could be either temporary or for the lifetime of the individual.” [851]

Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):

♠ physical punishment ♣ present ♥ Fasting was commonly included in the epitimia that were “imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession.” [852]
♠ exile ♣ inferred absent ♥ Based on the following quote, it seems that exile was a form of punishment that could be inflicted by the state, and not by religious agents—indeed, it was commonly inflicted ‘’upon’’ religious agents. “The Book of the Eparch several times mentions ‘’exoria’’ as a penalty for economic crimes; normally, however, exile was reserved for political criminals and suspects, esp. church leaders (John Chrysostom, Pope Martin I, Theodore of Stoudios, Photios, etc.).” [853] Excommunication “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist.” [854] but the relevant entry of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ does not say that this exclusion was physical.
♠ loss/seizure of property or wealth ♣ inferred absent ♥ Epitimia, commonly “imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession [...] were never reduced to a payment or a fine due to God.” [855] Similarly, the relevant entry of the ‘’Oxford Dictionary of Byzantium’’ [856] does not say that excommunication included the loss or seziure of property.
♠ loss of status ♣ present ♥ Excommunication “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist.” [857]

Extent of punishment (can be more than one):

♠ punishment of rulers ♣ present ♥ Excommunication “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist. Offending members included emperors, for example, Leo VI and Michael VIII.” [858]
♠ punishment of elites ♣ inferred present ♥ There were two types of punishment that religious agents or institutions could impose on the faithful: epitimia and excommunication. Because even emperors could be subjected to the latter, graver form of punishment, it seems reasonable to assume that elites could also be excommunicated or forced to undergo an epitimion. The epitimion was “a penalty imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession. The term was already in use by the 4th C. (Basil the Great, PG 32:721A). As a rule these penitential exercises, mentioned in Byz. canonical and ascetical literature, presupposed repentance and consisted of prayer, fasting, Scripture reading, prostrations, almsgiving, and, on occasion, temporary exclusion from the Eucharist. They were distinguished by their largely positive character and relative mildness from such formal punishments (‘’timoriai’’) as excommunication, suspension, or deposition, which were inflicted by the church for more serious transgressions such as heresy or apostasy. Since sin was understood as a disease rather than a legally punishable crime, ‘’epitimia’’ in Byz. penitential practice and theology were viewed as corrective remedies, that is, as a form of spiritual healing. At any rate, they were never reduced to a payment or a fine due to God.” [859] As for excommunication, it “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist. Offending members included emperors, for example, Leo VI and Michael VIII. The separation from the church’s sacramental life was either absolute or partial, that is, it could be either temporary or for the lifetime of the individual.” [860]
♠ punishment of commoners ♣ inferred present ♥ There were two types of punishment that religious agents or institutions could impose on the faithful: epitimia and excommunication. Because even emperors could be subjected to the latter, graver form of punishment, it seems reasonable to assume that commoners could also be excommunicated or forced to undergo an epitimion. The epitimion was “a penalty imposed on a penitent by the priest following sacramental confession. The term was already in use by the 4th C. (Basil the Great, PG 32:721A). As a rule these penitential exercises, mentioned in Byz. canonical and ascetical literature, presupposed repentance and consisted of prayer, fasting, Scripture reading, prostrations, almsgiving, and, on occasion, temporary exclusion from the Eucharist. They were distinguished by their largely positive character and relative mildness from such formal punishments (‘’timoriai’’) as excommunication, suspension, or deposition, which were inflicted by the church for more serious transgressions such as heresy or apostasy. Since sin was understood as a disease rather than a legally punishable crime, ‘’epitimia’’ in Byz. penitential practice and theology were viewed as corrective remedies, that is, as a form of spiritual healing. At any rate, they were never reduced to a payment or a fine due to God.” [861] As for excommunication, it “entailed the exclusion of the transgressor from the community or fellowship of the church and its sacraments, esp. the Eucharist. Offending members included emperors, for example, Leo VI and Michael VIII. The separation from the church’s sacramental life was either absolute or partial, that is, it could be either temporary or for the lifetime of the individual.” [862]

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ present ♥ Xenodocheion were used to organise a response to famine.[864] Present in some cases, but depending on the policy and means of the government.[865]

♠ Food storage ♣ present ♥ In the 6th century CE "Justinian built an enormous storehouse at the mouth of the Hellespont, 90 feet by 280 feet; and 'ineffably' high. ... There were also at least four other granaries dating from earlier times, and other cities such as Alexandria had their massive storehouses as well."[867]

♠ provision of food storage ♣ ♥

♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ present ♥ Xenodocheion (or ptochotropheia) were established by bishops and wealthy Christians to provide a place of rest and food to poor travellers and the homeless. They first appeared from the mid-fourth century CE.[868] "The tribute that Egypt paid was tribute in grain - about 240 metric tons per year. This vast amount of wheat was brought in to Constantinople for the bread dole - central to the extensive social welfare system in the city."[869]

♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ ♥

♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ ♥

♠ Health infrastructure ♣ present ♥ From 60 CE Jews built hostels for pilgrims to the Holy Land across the east Mediterranean (including Anatolia) which provided shelter and medical care. Also in the early first millennium the Church ruled that all Christian communities should have a spare room for the needy. These provisions grew in size and by 600 CE the largest ‘rooms’ had become highly specialised medical buildings with wards. The first Christian charity-funded operation that most resembled a hospital (nosokomeion) was established in 372 CE in Cappadocia. Specialised buildings for trained physicians and other carers would follow in Constantinople, Alexandria and other major cities across the empire and be maintained for at least a thousand years. Many were privately sustained, while others were established and maintained by the state.[870][871]

♠ Publicly funded or subsidized education ♣ inferred present ♥ Ecclesiastical schools took the place of universities closed in the sixth century: "the ecclesiastical patriarchal school in Constantinople played a role in the monastic world similar to that of the universities of Athens, Alexandria and Beirut in the world of late antiquity."[875] "The allocation of chairs showed that the university as it existed in the first half of the fifth century, had sunk to the level of an institution for professional training. The universal nature of a real university had been lost since the days of the Alexandrines. Here young men now received the education necessary to equip them for the higher offices in the civil service."[876] University of Constantinople closed in seventh century then reopened by Heraclius (r.610-641 CE), then closed in 730 CE. Refounded under Bardas Caesar (high official) in 860 CE.

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥

Revenues in the late Roman period "were largely based on a very comprehensive system of land-taxation, where land was graded according to its quality and use and the tax-payment fixed accordingly. The tax-registers of every fiscal unit were revised regularly. The system was inherited from the later Roman empire and, in spite of subsequent alterations, it reflects a large measure of administrative continuity. A large part of the superstructural apparatus of Byzantium had been carried over from the Roman empire."[878]

"The state was responsible for all issues of money, which it coined to meet its administrative and military expensive. It reclaimed the gold coinage through taxation."[879]

Between late Antiquity and Middle Ages: "The cities had been centres of local government, exacting revenues for themselves and the state from their territories. This institutional link between the cities and their rural areas was ruptured, resulting in a decline in the importance of the cities in social and economic terms."[880]

HPI Variables[edit]

♠ Dedicated police force ♣ present ♥ In Constantinople the chief of police was the city prefect - eparch - (also the chief justice), who was directly appointed by the emperor, who worked out of an office building called the Praetorium. His staff included a range of powerful officials whose responsibilities ranged from fortification to street cleaning, from maintenance for baths and the hippodrome to water management. They sponsored markets and churches, monitored the scales, measurements and weights, issued regulations for buildings and guilds, maintained public order and provided a response to fires. The Praetorium was also the main police station which was close to courts and a prison. Legatarioi were officials who had a role in policing. They wee appointed by the exarch (with the emperor’s approval). Their duties included interdiction of smuggled goods and registering foreigners who entered Constantinople and ensured they did not stay more than 3 months. Armed officers called 'collegiati' were present on the streets armed with clubs and maces.[881] [882] [883]
♠ Police levels ♣ 5 ♥ The Emperor (1) appointed an Exarch - city prefect - of Constantinople (2) who ran a specialist administrative building (Praetorium) which contained high officials who worked on many aspects of city life. There were likely offices within the Praetorium dedicated to security and/or law and order (3). High officials appointed by the exarch included the symponoi and legatarioi. Policing also involves white collar crimes like fraud so market inspection which may have been run out of a different office and had a separate chain of authority could be considered police work. There were probably sub-stations around and outside the city (4) as the exarch's authority extended beyond the city. At the lowest level were the 'collegiati' patrol men (5) who were armed with clubs and maces. The chain-of-command for market inspection is known to have five levels: Baileus - eparchos - symponos - legatarios - boullotes (the last in line policed instruments used for measuring and weighing and checked quantities that had the official seal).[884] [885] [886]
♠ Proscriptions of elites and or property ♣ ♥ Use of the state to repress or purge rival elite groups.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ ♥


♠ Military control over independent or semi-independent vassal states ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Were the Ghassanids still officially a vassal at this time? "the internal danger from the too-powerful military governors (strategi), whose loose dependence on the central power often resembles vassal relations, made it imperative to reduce the extensive stretches of territory under their rule.”[887]
